I.
Preface
This thesis is submitted as the ﬁnal product for the MSc degree in Architectural Engineering at
the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The report is produced as an equal collaboration
of the group members and corresponds to 30 ECTS points for each member.
Peter Noe Poulsen and Linh Cao Hoang have been main supervisors and John Forbes Olesen is
co-supervisor for this thesis.
A special thanks goes out to Tyr´ens A/S and their department in Herlev, Denmark, for making

the project.

II.
Abstract
In today’s world, business is moving fast. By the help of computers, it has been possible to
automate work processes to reduce time spent on a problem or a case. Such tools can sometimes
be essential to beat competitors and thereby save time and money. For post tensioned beams,
a lot of time can be spent trying to manually optimize a cable curve for a series of loads, with
a long line of design parameters.
This thesis focuses on how to create a numerical modeling of post tensioned beams and ways to
optimize the cable curve. By modeling post tensioned beams in FEM, the curve optimization
can be greatly sped up. It also adds the possibility of adjusting the jacking force needed in
the tension cable. By reducing the force needed, the amount of steel can be reduced in post
tensioned concrete beams.
The product of this report is a program, which can optimize the cable curve based on a ﬁxed
jacking force. It can also optimize the jacking force by adjusting it until a stress criterion in
the beam is reached, based on user input. The program is based on BygFEM ramme. Through
investigation of how the tension curve of a post tensioned beam interacts with the beam, the
original script has been transformed into the ﬁnal program over many iterations. The created
program can also be used to run a ﬁxed curve path with a predetermined jacking force to
calculate the sectional forces of a post tensioned beam. The program can calculate on beams
of any support conﬁguration, statically determinate beams, ﬁxed beams and multi-span beams
to name a few. Examples of optimizations of diﬀerent beams by the program are illustrated
within.

1.
Introduction
Concrete is a material which has very low tension strength. If a concrete structural element is
loaded by a shear force on one side, tension forces occur in the outer ﬁbers on the side. Steel
reinforced concrete beams increase the moment bearing capacity of concrete beams by allowing
the concrete beam to crack, and carry the tension forces in the steel and the compression forces
in the concrete.
In environments where it is undesirable that concrete beams have crack, but still need to carry
large shear loads, prestressing the concrete beams before other loads are applied is a solution.
The concrete beams do not need to deform before prestressing forces are activated in the beam,
unlike slack reinforcement. These environments include instances where the reinforced beams
would be exposed to chlorides on a daily basis, like bridge structure, and other road works.
When in-situ casting concrete beams, instead of prestressing the reinforcement strands before
pouring, the prestressing cables can be added after the concrete has cured. These are post
tension cables. Before the concrete is poured, tubes are added to the moulds which serve has
pathways for the post tension cables once the concrete is ready.
Post-tensioned concrete elements are used in diﬀerent types of building elements, but most of
them can be described using beam characteristics. Giant bi-directly uniform concrete slabs can
be simpliﬁed to a beam. The same holds true for horizontally loaded walls or columns. One
of the structures, where post-tensioned reinforcement is used the most, are in concrete bridge
decks. Post-tensioning is used to increase the length of spans of any type of bridge and can at
the same time increase the load bearing capacity of the structural elements that are prestressed.
Post tension cables in concrete work by applying a large compression force at the ends of the
element, and dependent on the path of the tubing, apply shear forces and moments in the beam
as well. For statically indeterminate post tensioned concrete beam systems, a valid path of the
cable can take a long time to design. To evaluate the best solution to the design, there are a lot
of parameters which can be tweaked. If they are being changed by hand, and then given as input
to a structural analysis program, it is a process which can take a long time. Comparatively,
if the optimal curve proﬁle can be calculated to a given load proﬁle, a lot of iterations can be
avoided, and the design process sped up.
1
==PAGE:14==
1.1. FINITE ELEMENT METHOD
The purpose of this project is to design a program able to do numerical modeling of the sectional
forces in simply supported as well as statically indeterminate post-tensioned beams. Besides
being able to analyse the forces applied to a beam by the post-tension cable, the program should
also be able to ﬁnd and return the optimal curve path of the post-tension cable. Being able
to ﬁnd most eﬀective path of the post-tension cable curve could result in being able to reduce
the total force needed to be applied to the beam. By ﬁnding the lowest cable force needed for
a system, this could reduce the overall steel used when constructing post-tensioned concrete
beams.
It is the aim of this thesis to investigate and explain the design decisions made during the cre-
ation of a program able to calculate sectional forces of post-tensioned concrete beams by using
numerical modeling. To prove the validity of the calculations done within the program, diﬀerent
simple cases will be compared between the program and analytical calculation methods. Diﬀer-
ent optimizers which utilize the section force calculation program will be presented and diﬀerent
tension curves for statically indeterminate systems calculated by the optimizer programs will
be presented and explained. Lastly, diﬀerent discrepancies between analytical calculations and
the numerical model are discussed along with possible changes and improvements.
1.1
Finite Element Method
The numerical modeling of post-tensioned beams performed in this project use the ﬁnite element
method to approximate a solution to a problem. For simple problems, such as simply supported
beams loaded with a uniform load, a solution can be found in a matter of minutes using analytical
equations with pen and paper calculations and CAS. When static systems become increasingly
statically indeterminate, using analytical equations become increasingly complex. FEM divides
the problem into smaller sub-problems, which can be solved rather easily. To do this eﬀectively,
the analytical equations have been converted to numerical approximations.
When using FEM in structural analysis, the models can range from very simple to very complex.
Entire building structures can be modeled and analyzed at the same time, or individual building
components can be analyzed to its smallest part. For a project like this, the problem is seen in 1
dimension. Here a beam can be seen as a straight line, which can be divided further into smaller
elements, like seen in ﬁgure 1.1. Each element has two end nodes, with corresponding degree
Figure 1.1: General topology of a straight beam spanning in 1D divided into 3 elements
2
==PAGE:15==
1.2. BASIS OF THE PROJECT
of freedoms (DOFs). Each DOF is equivalent to a sectional force applied at the node. As a
general rule of thumb, the error ratio of the calculations are proportional to how many elements
a system is divided into. There are some structural systems which can be approximated with
very few element divisions.
1.2
Basis of the project
To evaluate frame structures, the program BygFEM ramme was created by Peter Noe Poulsen
and John Forbes Olesen, two of our supervising professors. The program is used in the DTU
course Structural Engineering 1.
It can calculate the sectional forces in a frame structure,
based on manual creation of the topology, load distributions, material properties and supports.
There exists other, more complex versions of BygFEM created at the building institute at DTU,
though for the needs of this project, BygFEM ramme is a great basis for the project..
BygFEM ramme is built in a way where it is fairly easy to use as student of structural mechanics.
It follows the design basis of the ﬁnite element method. It includes a number of variables used to
create the situation’s geometry, loads and properties, and a number of sub-functions to calculate
the sectional forces and display them for the user.

2.
Theory
The theory described in this chapter is post tension cable force theory, including point loads due
to kinks, domain loads due to cable curvature and the losses in cable force over the cable length
from friction and anchor loss. The literature used for those sections, section 2.1 to 2.3 are [11]
and [6] and theory from the DTU course Advanced Concrete Structures which the authors of
this thesis has completed.
The optimizer used in the program is fminsearch, which is based on the Nelder-Mead algorithm.
Section 2.4 describes the Nelder-Mead algorithm and its implementation as the MATLAB func-
tion fminsearch as documented on the MathWorks website, [8][9].
2.1
Post tensioned cable forces
The post tension cables in concrete structures utilize the elastic yield stress of the steel. A
simple analogy are rubber bands. If a rubber band is cut in half and at each end pulled quite a
distance from each other, the system will be under stressed in tension. The rubber band will try
to contract, acting as an in-line force on whatever is keeping the ends them apart. If another
object is pressed down on the rubber band, orthogonally on the span direction, that object
will receive a force going the other direction, increasing in magnitude the farther the object is
displacing the span, thus increasing the stress.
The same phenomenon happens with the post tension cables. After they are inserted, they
are jacked to a predetermined stress level, often 80% of the elastic yield stress[11] (table 3.6).
If the tension curve is straight, there is nothing the steel will push against, apart from the
ends of the element. If the curve has a kink, a point where the curve is continuous but not
diﬀerentiable, a point load will occur. Seen in ﬁgure 2.1, if the cable curve has a kink, where
the angle to horizontal before and after the kink is the same size but diﬀerent sign, the resulting
force is applied in the point is twice the sine of the cable force. The right side pulls horizontally
with P cos ϕ and vertically with P sin ϕ, and the left side pulls horizontally with −P cos ϕ and
vertically with P sin ϕ.
5
==PAGE:18==
2.1. POST TENSIONED CABLE FORCES
Figure 2.1: Point load due to cable kink with mirrored angle
If the cable curve has no angle to horizontal before the kink, and an angle after the kink, the
resulting point load will have a vertical and a horizontal component, seen on ﬁgure 2.2. The
right side pulls horizontally with P cos ϕ and vertically with P sin ϕ and the left side pulls only
horizontally with −P. Thereby, the point load will pull left horizontally, and have a resulting
vertical component.
Figure 2.2: Point load due to cable kink with mirrored angle
These possible point loads are added in the program in 4.2.3.1 along with the forces applied at
the ends of the beam.
If the curve is continuous and diﬀerentiable over a distance, while still having a curvature ̸= 0,
a distributed force is applied over that distance, see ﬁgure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Forces shown on a cut out section of the reinforcement cable under tensioning
The contact force from the tension curve, can be divided into vertical and horizontal components.
The magnitude of these forces are proportional with the jacking force and the curve angle to
horizontal.
6
==PAGE:19==
2.2. FRICTION LOSS
The vertical component of the cable domain load becomes
pv = P · cos ϕ · 1
R
(2.1)
The horizontal component of the cable domain load becomes
ph = P · sin ϕ · 1
R
(2.2)
Where R is the radius of curvature of the curve, P is the force in the cable and ϕ is the angle
of the curve to horizontal. The radius of curvature can be rewritten as 1
R = κ, the curvature of
the the curve.
Over the length of a post tensioned beam with a parabolic curve, the sectional forces can be
approximated to
N(x) = −P cos ϕ(x)
(2.3)
Q(x) = −P sin ϕ(x)
(2.4)
M(x) = −P cos ϕ(x) · z(x)
(2.5)
Where x is the distance from the left side of the beam and z(x) is the height between the
center of the cable curve and the center of the beam. The forces described in equation 2.1
and equation 2.2 are added to the program as described in section 4.2.3.2. The sectional forces
approximations are used to validate the program in section 5.1.
2.2
Friction loss
The cable force is not constant through the length of the cable duct. Because the cable rubs
against the duct when is is getting stressed, friction loss is observed throughout the length of
the cable. The diﬀerence in cable force is between the cable force at the anchorage point before
locking it in and the force in the ﬁxed end. This friction is calculated from two diﬀerent causes.
The angle change induced by the curvature of the cable path is one part of the friction loss.
The other part, is the wobble eﬀect of the duct. The friction happens instantly, when the post
tension cable is tightened and may be the greatest factor causing loss in the cable force. From
Equation 2.6 the friction loss over a given length can be calculated.
P(x) = P0 · e−(µ·ϕx+k·s(x))
(2.6)
7
==PAGE:20==
2.2. FRICTION LOSS
Where:
P
=
Presstressing force after friction loss
P0
=
Stress in tension cable at the anchorage point
µ
=
Coeﬃcient of angular friction between tension cable and its duct
κ
=
Wobble coeﬃcient per unit length
ϕ
=
Change in angle between anchorage and distance x
s(x)
=
Length of the cable curve to beam length x
x
=
Beam length from anchor point
The coeﬃcient of friction µ depends on the material and the type of cable used. The wobble
coeﬃcient κ deals with unintentional angular displacement. For small angle changes, the dis-
tance along the cable, s(x), is approximately equal to the projected horizontal distance along
the x axis. Figure 2.4 shows a cable jacked from both ends.
Figure 2.4: Forces on a reinforcement cable under tension from both ends
2.2.1
Wobble eﬀect
The wobble eﬀect is caused by irregularities in the duct and tension cable. It is only in theory
that a perfectly duct which follows the set path perfectly is constructed. In practise the duct
would not be perfect, causing a slight unintentional change in angle. These angle changes results
in irregularities, thus causing friction between the duct and the tension cable. The wobble eﬀect
is therefore a coeﬃcient that is applied to the friction formula equation 2.6, to compensate for
this eﬀect. In ﬁgure 2.5, the irregularities in the duct is illustrated causing the wobble eﬀect.
Figure 2.5: Wobbling eﬀect for a curved tendon
8
==PAGE:21==
2.3. ANCHORAGE LOSS
2.3
Anchorage loss
After the steel cable is tensioned and elongated, it has to be locked in place so it doesn’t contract
again. This is done by adding an anchor at the jacking ends. However, in this procedure it
requires that the wedge used for anchors ”sets in” a certain distance in order to lock the tendon
in place. The distance that the anchor ”sets in”, is dependent on the type of wedge used. A
typical distance for the anchor to displace, can vary between 4-10mm [1], section 2.6. This
wedge set-in causes the stress in tendon to drop, so the initial stress P0 is reduced. However,
due to friction between the tendon and the duct, the eﬀect from anchorage loss will decrease to
zero at a certain distance from the jacking end. The eﬀect of anchorage set, can be determined
by equation 2.7. This equation, compared to equation 2.6 is exponentially increasing. This is
because the set-in loss in cable force at the anchor gets less and less as it moves through the
cable, because of friction acting as a stopper, the direct opposite direction as friction due to the
jacking.
P = P0 · eµ·ϕ+k·x
(2.7)
The loss in cable force at the anchor due to the set-in can also be described by Hook’s law.
σp = Epϵ
(2.8)
The stress can also be expressed by the following equation, if the member is in pure compression
or tension, without any moment contribution.
σp = P
Ap
(2.9)
Hook’s law can be rewritten as
∆P
Ap = Epϵ
(2.10)
∆P = EpϵAp
(2.11)
By having ϵ as the anchorage set distance, s, the equation becomes
∆P = EpAp
s
lsl
(2.12)
In SI units, this is
N = N

(2.13)

any change in P(x) over a length can be explained as an area loss. Using equation 2.12, the
total area loss due to anchorage draw-in is EpAps The slope of the anchorage loss is the slope
of the friction loss times -1. Knowing the friction loss, the distance along the beam where the
anchorage loss graph hits the friction loss graph, as shown on ﬁgure 4.13 can be found by

lsl =

2.4
Nelder-Mead simplex algorithm
In MATLAB, it is possible to ﬁnd a minimum of a multivariable function in a derivative free
method. Fminsearch is a nonlinear program solver, which searches for a minimum of a speciﬁed
problem. To ﬁnd such a minimum the function fminsearch uses an algorithm called Nelder-Mead
simplex algorithm [8][9].
The algorithm starts by making a simplex around the initial input variable x0 by adding 5%
of each component x0(i) to x0 and thereby creating n vectors as elements of the simplex in
additional to x0.
From the initial input variable, the algorithm modiﬁes the simplex repeatedly according to the
below described procedure.
1. First x0(i) denotes a list of points in the current simplex, like so i = 1,. . . ,n+1.
2. The list is now ordered from lowest function value f(x(1)) to highest f(x(n+1)). At each
step in the iteration, the algorithm discards the current worst point x(n + 1), and accepts
a new point into the simplex.
3. Generate the reﬂected point
r = 2m −x(n + 1)
where
m = P x(i)/n,
i = 1. . . n,
and calculate f(r).
4. If f(x(1)) ≤f(r) < f(x(n)), the algorithm accepts r and terminates this iteration. fmin-
search iteration is displayed as Reﬂect.
5. If f(r) < f(x(1)), calculate the expansion point s
s = m + 2(m −x(n + 1))
and calculate f(s). From here one of the two statements can occur.
(a) If f(s) < f(r), accept s and terminate the iteration. fminsearch displays Expand.
(b) Else, accept r and terminate the iteration. fminsearch displays Reﬂect.
10
==PAGE:23==
2.4. NELDER-MEAD SIMPLEX ALGORITHM
6. If f(r) ≥f(x(n)), perform a contraction between m and the better of x(n + 1) and r.
Again two thinkable outcome are possible.
(a) If f(r) < f(x(n + 1)), (e.g. r is better than x(n + 1)), calculate c
c = m + (r −m)/2
and calculate f(c). If f(c) < f(r), accept c and terminate the iteration. fminsearch
displays Contract outside.
(b) If f(r) ≥f(x(n + 1)), calculate cc
Cc = m + (x(n + 1) −m)/2
and calculate f(cc). If f(cc) < f(x(n + 1)), accept cc and terminate the iteration.
fminsearch displays Contract inside.
If 4a and 4b are not fulﬁlled, the algorithm proceeds to step 5. Here it changes all n points
with value above f(x(1)).
7. Calculate the n points
v(i) = x(1) + (x(i) −x(1))/2
and calculate f(v(i)),
i = 2, . . . , n + 1. fminsearch displays Shrink and starts over
from the top again.
Figure 2.6: points that fminsearch might calculate in the procedure, along with each possible new simplex[9]
This iteration process continues until the algorithm is satisﬁed, meaning it has minimized the
function to a local minimum, or reaches a stopping criteria. These stopping criteria include
the a limit on the number of function calls or iterations the function can perform in a given
fminsearch call. An example of the adjustments to an input variable of the length four, can
be seen in ﬁgure 2.7. Here, four curve heights are adjusted based on an early version of the
program in this report.
11
==PAGE:24==
2.4. NELDER-MEAD SIMPLEX ALGORITHM
Figure 2.7: fminsearch iterations and the values of the input variable
The implementation of fminsearch in the program are described in section 4.5 and 4.6.

==PAGE:25==
3.
Early work
In the beginning of the program, a single quadratic curve was implemented as the post ten-
sioned cable proﬁle. This tension curve was perfect for simply supported beams with a constant
vertically distributed load. However, to create optimum curve proﬁles for static indeterminate
systems with multiple spans, supports, and diﬀerent load distributions required a further devel-
opment of the program. Instead of a single curve for the entire length, the curve was divided
into curve segments.
With each curve segment as a quadratic curve, running a curve optimizer guessing the curve
height at certain points created some problems. The curve segments were created between every
element node, and three curve heights for every curve segment was used to calculate the segment
equation. Based on theory, to completely negate the external vertical forces on a beam, a post
tension curve with the same shape as the moment curve would do just that. This meant that
for statically indeterminate beam, the post tension curve should have a kink at the support,
which would apply a vertical load into the support. These kinks did occur, and to correctly
model the forces resulting from them, additional forces were added in every node, see section
4.2.3.1.
These curve segments were pretty good for simply supported beams, when it was divided into
two or three curve segments. The curve optimizer was also able to create good curves for the
statically indeterminate 2-span beams with two or four curve segments. Problems arose when
trying to use more curve segments than those in beam systems, or having the beam supported
more than with four supports.
Figure 3.1: 26 curve segments, simply supported beam without curve bounds run through an early curve opti-
mizer. First optimized as linear segments and then converted to quadratic curve segments to be further optimized
13
==PAGE:26==
The curve ﬁtter was unable to create a curve which was diﬀerentiable in every point, even for
a simply supported system. A smooth curve was preferred for a simply supported system with
a distributed load. This means that no kinks or irregularities should be presents in the ﬁnal
curve proﬁle. Likewise, the only kinks which should occur in a 2-span beam should be over the
support, and this was not the case for the ﬁrst version of the program.
To help the curve optimizer, a new method for ﬁnding the best curve was devised.
First,
instead of using quadratic functions for the curve segments, straight lines between each element
node would create the curve path. Linear curve segments would not have any resulting load
application due to the curvature, but would apply loads only due to their change in angle in
the element nodes. Optimizing with linear curve segments did produce reliable ﬁtted curve
paths. However, wanting to do the calculations with a distributed load applied by the post
tension cable, the linear ﬁtted curve path used as a basis for an optimizer for the quadratic
curve segments. After optimizing with linear curve segments, those curve segments were then
converted into the quadratic curve segments, hoping to get a smooth diﬀerentiable curve between
supports.
Even though this improved the program, though still far from running as smooth as it should in
regards to curve ﬁtting, another issue was observed. When optimizing the curve for a statically
indeterminate beam which had a vertical support in the center diﬀerent valid solutions were
found for the system. Depending on number of curve elements, the curve would have the same
curvature in the curve up to the support, but the curves would start with diﬀerent angle to the
horizontal center line.
Based on these diﬃculties, and reading and learning from professionals in within the ﬁeld, the
curve segments were changed from the quadratic curve segments to cubic curve segments. Cubic
curve segments are deﬁned by four equations to ﬁnd the four coeﬃcients of the function, see
section 4.2.2.1. Sharing the height and slope in a point between each curve segment creates
a smooth curve for the entire length of the combined curve. This does by default make the
curve less optimized, since it would not be able to have any kinks, even if it would want them
to counteract the moment distribution. In return, the curves this program would be creating
are better suited to real world conditions, where cable tubing, and the cables within them are
unable to bend more than of a radius of curvature of much less than 7 meters, depending on
exact speciﬁcations.
14
==PAGE:27==
4.
Program creation
The program presented in this chapter is based a the scrip for FEM calculations of a frame
structure, BygFEM Ramme. BygFEM Ramme is used by DTU students in the course Structural
Engineering 1. Using BygFEM Ramme as a foundation, a program to to calculate the sectional
forces, top and bottom ﬁber stresses as well as optimize cable curves for a post-tensioned beam
was created. The following sections describes the idea behind each change or addition to the
foundational script, what it is supposed to do, and why. In each subsection, a reference to the
corresponding code is made to its place in the appendix.
BygFEM ramme is written in MATLAB, a programming language, which excels at matrix
operations, plottools, has optimization tools, and a lot of built-in functions. Therefore, it has
been optimal to continue using MATLAB when coding the program for this project, though
with adequate programming knowledge, it can be created in other languages as well.
For our program, a MATLAB package has been found on the MATLAB ﬁle exchange [10], which
is not standard for the student academic license of MATLAB. This ﬁle is an expansion of the
standard function optimizer fminsearch, fminsearchbnd. fminsearchbnd can bound the input
vairable used by fminsearch to keep it form exceeding a lower or upper bound. The function
fminsearch and how it works is discussed in section 2.4 and its implementation in this program
in sections 4.5 and 4.6. Whenever fminsearch is mentioned in the following sections, it is to be
understood that the same applies to fminsearchbnd.
The ﬁnal program consists of a number of separate scripts and functions.
At its core is
FEM CurveCore which can, given a vector of curve heights and slopes to speciﬁc points in
the beam element along with loads and beam topology, calculate the sectional forces in a sys-
tem. A curve optimizer has been created which relies on that core function. The optimizer
changes the curve height and slope to given x-values along the beam in an attempt to bring
the sum of all the moment values in the element nodes down as low as possible. On top of
this optimizer, another optimizer has been created. It varies the total force the post-tension is
jacked to until a stress criteria is minimized.
Each of these parts were created from the core outward, and can therefore be used as such. The
program can either run one tension curve through the core, optimize the tension curve for one
value of the total jacking force, or continually change the jacking force which requires a new
15
==PAGE:28==
curve to be found for every adjustment is makes to the input variable until a stress value is
reached in the top or bottom ﬁbers of the beam.
The program is divided into a number of function ﬁles, so they can be used without having
to call unnecessary functions. Figure 4.1 shows the function ﬂow of the program created to
optimize the jacking force by creating an optimum curve in the beam.
Figure 4.1: Jacking force optimizer
If the user just needs a program to optimize the curve proﬁle for a post tensioned beam with
a speciﬁed jacking force, the sectional forces can be calculated by running the Curveﬁtter.m
script which runs the functions shown in ﬁgure 4.2.
Figure 4.2: Curve optimizer
If the user needs a program which calculates the sectional forces in a beam with a ﬁxed curve
proﬁle and jacking force, the user can run SingleRun.m script which runs the functions shown
in ﬁgure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Fixed curve and jacking force
16
==PAGE:29==
4.1. STATIC DATA FUNCTION - SETELEMENTDATA.M
In the following sections and subsections, the diﬀerent function and variable constructions are
explained. The individual ﬁles described in the following sections relations can be seen in ﬁgure
4.1, ﬁgure 4.2, and ﬁgure 4.3.
4.1
Static data function - setElementData.m
This function, setElementData, was created to reduce the overall runtime of any of the optimizer
scripts. In the script BygFEM ramme the beam topology was created in the beginning of the
script.
This works great for scripts run just once, but not when used in an optimizer like
fminsearch, which adjusts a parameter and calls the core function again and again as described
in section 4.5.
With some systems run through fminsearch, the core function was running
millions of times. Creating the same beam topology every time the core function is run in an
optimizer is not needed since the topology is the same every time. Therefore, it has been moved
from the core function into a separate function.
The function setElementData.m creates the the topology of the beam. It does this based on an
input of the number of elements wanted to represent each curve segment, nelPrNcu. It creates
matrices of the following variables:
• Node placement in {x,y}, X.
• Horizontal placement of the nodes which act as curve nodes {x}, XK.
• Nodes which make up a single element, T.
• The global number assigned to the Degree of Freedoms each element consist of, D.
• The global DOFs which are supports, U.
The function also creates single value variables, like height, width and length of the beam.
Figure 4.4: Established axis and geometry
17
==PAGE:30==
4.1. STATIC DATA FUNCTION - SETELEMENTDATA.M
Figure 4.4 shows how the axis is established in the program for a post tensioned beam. The
beam geometry starts with the left end at origo and ending in (a, 0). The horizontal x axis is
divides the beam in half, where is curve under the center is at a negative value of y, and above
the center at a positive value of y.
The matrices built in setElementData were built by manually entering each row in BygFEM ramme.
Since the current beams only extend in one dimension, all these matrices can be built paramet-
rically. To build the topology of the system, the user needs to manually change some variables
in setElementData.m, described in section 4.1.1
4.1.1
User input to setElementData.m
To try to bring the number of places where the user needs to change data to ﬁt a desired beam
problem down, all the topology data which can be changed needs to be input in this ﬁle. This
includes the height of the beam, the width of the beam and the length of the beam. This also
includes the locations and local directions of the supports.
4.1.1.1
Macro dimensions - a, b, and h
When inputting data for element length, a, width, b, and height, h, it is important to make
sure that they are written in the correct unit. In this program, every variable is in SI units,
this includes distances [m], forces [N], moments [Nm] and stresses [Pa]. Keeping everything in
SI units has made it easier to directly error check without needing to convert the variables to
match analytical calculations. It also makes it easier to keep all the input data in the correct
magnitude relative to each other. The user can choose to change this, though be aware that
some forces, like the self weight of the beam, applied in the core script, see section 4.2.3.2,
assume everything is in meters and would need to be manually changed to reﬂect the user’s
choice. A problem which might occur at by changing the factors, is in regards to the curve
calculation. MATLAB uses 16 digits precision, which can cause inaccuracies, see section 7.5.
These variables can be seen in appendix A.1, line 3-5.
4.1.1.2
Support placements - Udist
The user must manually create a list of the supports that are present in the structural beam
system that they are using the program to evaluate. This is done by creating a list on the form
[local DOF, distance]. Local DOF should be a number between 1 to 3. These numbers indicate
the direction of the local freedom the beam is supported in. Writing a 1 creates an inﬁnitely
stiﬀsupport horizontally, 2 is vertical and 3 is a moment stiﬀsupport. distance indicates the
distance from the left end of the beam. It is advised to write it as a fraction of the total length
variable, a, though inputting the distance as a number is also possible, though. It will cause an
18
==PAGE:31==
4.1. STATIC DATA FUNCTION - SETELEMENTDATA.M
error if distance is greater than the value of a, the length of the beam. This is because it is not
possible to have a support of the beam beyond the end of the beam. The creation of the user
input supports can be seen in appendix A.1, line 8-15.
4.1.2
Element nodes and tension curve nodes, X and XK
The matrices of node placement and curve node placements are automatically generated to
reduce the number of inputs needed to be changed by the user. Both XK and by extension X
are solely based on the number of supports and their placement in the static system.
The creation of the post tension curve segments, are described in section 4.2.2.
The curve
segments are based on the x-value of the curve node, the y-value at that node and the slope
in the point. Creating more curve segments, increases the possible ways the curve path can
deform. To facilitate this, suﬃcient curve nodes should be created to help the curve optimizer
reduce the moment distribution in the beam, by changing the curve proﬁle.
Having a curve node on each support ensures the curve has the ability to conform to the
moment distribution of statically indeterminate beam structures. Furthermore, increase the
curve optimizers ability to tailor the curve near the supports, an additional curve node is placed
on either side of the supports, at a distance of dX = h. This distance was chosen to limit the
curvature, so that the maximum curvature, which is at a stationary point, of the tension curve
to be less than 1/7. With a distances of minimum h between each node it limits the possible
curve segment paths possible between each node. A curve with a radius of curvature less than
7 are not used in practise. Therefore, by having a minimum distance between curve nodes,
the radius of curvature of the curve is never much smaller than this. Having the curve in this
program have a radius of curvature which is much smaller than 7m is unrealistic, even though
it would minimize the moment even better. Having the distance between each curve node be
very small also leads to other inaccuracies, described in section 7.5.
To increase the accuracy of the curve optimizer further a curve node at the middle distance
between each support is added as well. This is to be able to bound the height of the curve to
prevent it from going outside the dimensions of the beam, see section 4.5.1. For beams with
more than two supports, the distance between this middle point and the point near the left and
right side of a support needs to be checked to make sure it is not too short, less than h. If this
is the case, one of these curve nodes are removed (never placed) in the XK list. XK is created
in line 23-55 of setElementData, seen in appendix A.1.
The element nodes, X, are saved as {x,y} values. It is a byproduct of BygFEM ramme. This
program has been created with one-dimensional beams in mind and therefore every y-value of
every element node in X is 0. In general, structures with great complexity needs more elements
than a structure which is simple. To increase accuracy of this program a number of element
nodes are created between each curve node.
The amount varies based on the input to the
19
==PAGE:32==
4.1. STATIC DATA FUNCTION - SETELEMENTDATA.M
setElementData, nelPrNcu. If no input is given, the default value is set to 4. This means that
four elements are created between each curve node. Thereby, XK is included in X ; as well
as three points placed equal distance between every XK. Two examples of this can be seen in
Figure 4.5. To make sure the nodes are named in the correct order, both X and XK are sorted
by distance from left to right. X is created in lines 65-73 i nappendix A.1. The beam depicted
in the left in ﬁgure 4.5, a simple supported beam is modelled with two curve segments. On the
ﬁgure to the right, a statically indeterminate beam is modelled with 6 curve segments.
Figure 4.5: Topology of two diﬀerent structural systems
After XK and X are found, these two variables are used to deﬁne two values used throughout
the scripts, ncu and nel. The variable ncu is the number of curve segments used on the beam
and nel is the number of elements the beam is divided into. These are deﬁned as the number of
rows in XK minus 1, and the number of rows in X minus 1, size(XK) −1 and size(X, 1) −1
respectively. See line 58 and 63 in appendix A.1.
4.1.3
Element topology and degree of freedom, T and D
After the list of element nodes coordinates has been created the elements themselves, T, are
created. In BygFEM ramme the elements needed to be created manually since the elements
could span from node 3 to 6, 4 to 1, and the like. This is no longer the case because the beams
in this program only extend in one dimension. Now, the matrix which keeps element node
information is created through consecutive nodes, 1 to 2, 2 to 3, and so forth. This can be seen
in appendix A.1 line 76-80.
Degree of freedom, DOF, tells something about the way individual nodes can move. It is through
the interactions in DOFs that individual elements check if they interact with each other when
calculating sectional forces in the structure. They are locally deﬁned from 1 to 3, where 1 is
horizontal, 2 is vertical and 3 is the moment. The direction of the DOFs can be seen in ﬁgure
20
==PAGE:33==
4.1. STATIC DATA FUNCTION - SETELEMENTDATA.M
4.6
(a) Illistration showing one element and two
nodes
(b) Global DOF direction and section forces for
each node
Figure 4.6: DOF system
The global degree of freedom information in each element is also created consecutively. This is
because each node has a maximum of two elements connected to it. Element 1 should have the
DOFs [1,2,3] for node X (1,:) and DOFs [4,5,6] for node X (2,:). The next element, element 2,
then consists of DOFs [4,5,6,7,8,9]. This continues until every element is deﬁned by the DOFs
it interacts with. This can be seen in appendix A.1 line 83-87.
4.1.4
Supports - U
The core function, which can be seen in appendix A.2, uses the global DOF numbers when
applying supports. The topology of the beam is automatically generated based on the location
of supports, the amount of them, and their relative distance to each other. This makes it near
impossible to know beforehand what the global DOFs are for each support. This is also why the
user manually enters the support location as a distance and the local DOF instead, see section
4.1.1.2.
From the generated element nodes, X, and the manually entered support distances, it is possible
to create a vector of the global DOFs which are supported. This is done by ﬁnding each of the
element nodes in X, which are at the same distance as the needed supports, and then converting
the local DOF provided in Udist to the global one. To make sure that the same support is not
listed twice, any duplicate rows in Udist are removed before ﬁnding the global DOFs. The
creation of the support vector U can be seen in appendix A.1 line 91-96
21
==PAGE:34==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
4.2
Core - FEM CurveCore.m
The core script, ”FEM CurveCore” is a function call, which accepts all the diﬀerent outputs of
setElementData, as well as load variables. The load variables are P0, which is the maximum
jacking force in the beam, and pl, which is the uniformed distributed load on the beam. It
also receives the vector of the height and slope in each curve node, YKg. It needs every single
variable for it to run. To run a ﬁxed curve case with a set jacking force through the section force
calculator, a small script, SingleRun.m as seen in ﬁgure 4.2, with calls to both setElementData
and the core function has been created. The script can be seen in appendix A.12. The beam
geometry input does not need to come from setElementData. If the user can create their own to
run beam geometry to run through the program, if setElementData does not create the desired
geometry.
The core function is based on the BygFEM ramme script provided at the start of this project.
The following sections will illustrate the changes made to the original script. The function
consists of all the calculations and variables which are aﬀected by the path of the tension curve.
The only thing which does not change based on the moment curve which is still deﬁned in
this function are the material properties of the beam. There are many ways one could move
them out of the core and into a function like setElementData, but since the calculations of the
materials are single value variables, these are at the bottom of the list in terms of CPU time.
If they were to be moved, it would be to ease the layout for the user.
The following sections in 4.2 describe the changes made to BygFEM ramme to calculate the
curve path from the given curve node values and implements its aﬀects on the beam.
4.2.1
Fixes and changes to BygFEM ramme
Apart from additions and changes to BygFEM ramme due to the addition of post tension
cables, a few ﬁxes were performed on the original script ﬁrst. A runtime improvement was
found nessecary. It was notable once the core script was running thousand of cycles inside an
fminsearch optimizer. The way uniformly distributed loads were added to each element was
ﬁxed as well.
4.2.1.1
Runtime improvement
The runtime improvement was the removal of a MATLAB handle called dot(n,n) which would
calculate the scalar product of a vector multiplication. This was used to normalize directional
vectors.
This handle was not needed though, since the vectors in question were only two
dimensional. This was replaced with n12 + n22, improving the speed of that calculation from
201 seconds to ∼0.4 seconds for 10 million dot(n,n) runs on the same machine. This was a
22
==PAGE:35==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
handle which had over 46 million calls in one of the optimization runs early in the programming
phase. It was called so many times because dot was called every time Abeam and S was called.
This is the only change to Abeam while S was changed a bit more below.
4.2.1.2
Fixing application of distributed load
One of the reasons to use BygFEM ramme as a foundation for the program, was that it already
had the ability to convert distributed loads, also called domain loads dL, into node forces.
The loads were written in dL(el,:)=[local DOF, load size]. This had two issues, which made it
diﬃcult to use eﬀectively for the program.
The ﬁrst was, that each dL was tied to an element, instead of just being deﬁned by the number
of individual distributed loads the user wanted to add. Apart from it creating more work for
the user, having to add all the loads together before adding them to an element, it limited the
amount of distributed loads on the element to either horizontal or vertical load, not both at
the same time. It wouldn’t cause much of an issue in normal use, but for adding the horizontal
aﬀects of the tension curve forces, both needed to be present at the same time on the same
element.
Due to this, dL was changed to dL(i,:)=[el, local DOF, load size], and then all of dL was
consolidated into another matrix dLn, which had rows equal to the number of elements and two
columns. The ﬁrst column for horizontal distributed forces and the second column for vertical
distributed forces. Every call of dL was then changed in the subsequent script to match the way
dLn was built. This needed to be done in the load vector R and in the calculation of sectional
forces, S.
The other issue was, that if dL consisted of horizontal loads, only the sectional forces were
calculated correct, but the plot of the sectional forces, plotS, was not. Since plotS is still used
in this program, this was changed to work by changing how the distributed load was called, so
it would plot the correct data.
4.2.2
Creation of the curve
A major addition to the core script is the creation of the tension curve geometry. It is created
by an input to the core function of all the heights and the slopes of the curve at all the curve
nodes. It is a vector on the form Y Kg = [y1, h1, y2, h2, ..., yncu, hncu]. To be able to plot the
curve based on the data in this input, a series of steps needs to be performed on the data,
explained in the following subsections.
23
==PAGE:36==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
4.2.2.1
Curve parameters and coeﬃcients - kp & getCurve
As mentioned in earlier sections, the tension curve in the beam is built of a series of individual
cubic curve segments. These curve segments span two consecutive curve nodes. For each cubic
function, given as f(x) = ax3 + bx2 + cx + d, the coeﬃcients {a,b,c,d} needs to be calculated.
Since these are four variables, four equations are needed to isolate each variable. These four
equations are found by solving the cubic function for the height in two curve nodes, and the
slope, f′(x), in the same nodes.
y1 = ax13 + bx12 + cx1 + d
(4.1)
h1 = 3ax12 + 2bx1 + c
(4.2)
y2 = ax23 + bx22 + cx2 + d
(4.3)
h2 = 3ax22 + 2bx2 + c
(4.4)
The coeﬃcients {a,b,c,d} need to be found for each cubic function which start at one curve
node and ends at the next curve node. The matrix kp is created to handle that. Each row
consists of all the data needed to create a cubic function between two curve nodes, kp(i, :) =
[xki , yi , hi , xki+1 , yi+1 , hi+1], where i is the curve node number. Each new curve segment
starts at the same point as the previous curve segment ends, and with the same slope. The
transition between each curve segment is smooth, without any kinks. This was not the case
when each curve segment was linear elements or even with quadratic functions, see section 3.
Equations 4.1 to 4.4 are isolated for the coeﬃcients {a,b,c,d} by CAS, giving the following
solutions.
a = h1x1−h1x2+h2x1−h2x2−2y1+2y2
(x1−x2)3
(4.5)
b = −h1x12+h1x1x2−2h1x22+2h2x12−h2x1x2−h2x22−3x1y1+3x1y2−3x2y1+3x2y2
(x1−x2)3
(4.6)
c = 2h1x12x2−h1x1x22−h1x23+h2x13+h2x12x2−2h2x1x22−6x1x2y1+6x1x2y2
(x1−x2)3
(4.7)
d = −h1x12x22−h1x1x23+h2x13x2−h2x12x22−x13y2+3x12x2y2−3x1x22y1+x23y1)
(x1−x2)3
(4.8)
Where x1 = kp(i, 1), y1 = kp(i, 2), h1 = kp(i, 3), x2 = kp(i, 4), y2 = kp(i, 5), h2 = kp(i, 6).
These {a,b,c,d} are calculated for each element in the beam, creating a matrix of ABCD values,
where each row is a new element.
Because each curve runs through nelPrNcu number of
elements, the values in ABCD are the same for the same number of rows at a time. The ABCD
values calculated for each curve segment can be used to calculate the height of the tension curve
for any point needed on the beam, or to be exported into CAS or spreadsheets for a use in other
graphing tools. As an example, ABCD are used to calculate the variable YK, see appendix A.2
24
==PAGE:37==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
line 32-42 and 404-416, which calculates the tension curve height in each element node. It is
also used to calculate the curve slope angle and the curve curvature for each node and element.
4.2.2.2
Angle - ϕ(x)
To be able to calculate the forces exerted on the beam by the tension curve, the angle between
the curve tangent at a point and the horizontal axis needs to be calculated.
Figure 4.7: Found angle from diﬀerent curves with diﬀerent slope
Illustrated in 4.7, the angle the tension curve tangent makes with horizontal, can be found in
a geometrically similar triangle with the base of 1, the same angle, and the opposite side being
the change of height, z′(x), see ﬁgure 4.8. This is the same as the deﬁnition of tan(ϕ).
Figure 4.8: Calculation of angle
Therefore,
phi(x) = tan−1(z′(x))
(4.9)
To be able to calculate the angle of the curve to each element node, the {a,b,c,d} found in
4.2.2.1 are used. To ﬁnd f′(x) it is possible to calculate symbolic in MATLAB. Doing so adds
unimaginable amount of time to the total runtime, due to the amount of calls in each run
through of the core script. The much faster solution is writing the derivative manually.
phi(x0) = tan−1(3ax02 + 2bx0 + c)
(4.10)
25
==PAGE:38==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
The angles are calculated in FEM CurveCore in a subfunction, seen in appendix A.2 line 363-378
and called in line 45-50 of FEM CurveCore.
4.2.2.3
Curvature - κ(x)
Curvature of a 2D curve is given by 4.11.
κ =
z′′
(1 + z′2)
3
2
(4.11)
Because of the way the the post tension curve applies forces to the beam, see section 4.2.3, the
curvature of the each curve segment is needed. Since each curve segment is a cubic function, it
does not have a constant curvature, unlike if the tension curve was made of circle segments and
straight lines. With the way the curvature is used in section 4.2.3.2, a value for the curvature
over an entire element is required. This is done by calculating the curvature for multiple x-
values on an element evenly split calculating the average of them. This gives the most accurate
representation of the curvature over a distance of the tension curve without having to resort to
inﬁnitely small beam elements in the program for a complete picture. Equation 4.11 for a cubic
function becomes
κ(x0) =
6ax0 + 2b
(1 + (3ax02 + 2bx0 + c)2)
3
2
(4.12)
The curvatures are calculated in FEM CurveCore in a subfunction, seen in appendix A.2 line
381-401 and called in line 53-60 of FEM CurveCore.
4.2.3
Adding curve forces to the beam
In this section the forces which are applied to the beam due to the cable are described. All the
forces applied to the beam by the tension curve depend on P0. P0 is the jacking force exerted
on the steel before the anchor is set. Additionally, the forces are directly dependent on the path
of the tension curve. There are two types of loads applied to the beam in the core function.
There are boundary loads, bL, which are point loads applied directly to the DOFs in an element
node. There are also the possibility of adding distributed loads, dL, which are then converted
to equivalent point loads in the calculations.
4.2.3.1
Boundary load - bL
The boundary loads are loads applied directly to the element nodes in their global DOF. Due
to the tension curve, there are six loads that are applied to the system ﬁrst.
26
==PAGE:39==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
• There is a normal force in the start and end node.
• There is a shear force in the start and end node, if the curve starts at an angle.
• There is a moment in the start and end node, due to curve eccentricity.
When applying the forces described above, it is important to make sure they are applied with
the correct sign + or -, depending on the desired direction of the force and the direction of the
DOF. The DOF directions can be seen in section 4.1.3, ﬁgure 4.6.
For every element node, additional forces are applied as well.
These were added when the
curve still was created of quadratic or linear curve segments. Every kink the curve could have
would result in a shear force and a normal force being applied to the beam. These were then
calculated and added to every node based on the curve path. Furthermore, A resulting moment
was not added, as the only way that could be applied to the beam would be if the curve was
discontinues. Otherwise, adding it would just make it equal out with a moment contributed by
the curve just on the other side of the node. These nodal forces due to kinks in the curve are
still being calculated in the program, but are not needed since not only is the curve continuous
in the entire length of beam, it is also diﬀerentiable in the entire beam. Thereby, all the forces
calculated for the tension curve just before and right after a node cancel each other out. The
forces are added like seen in ﬁgure 4.9.
Figure 4.9: DOFs with applied loads for a two element system
It is also in this matrix where the user manually enters any point load or point moments the
beam problem calls for. The matrix should be written as bL(i,:)=[global DOF, size]. The size
is in newton, and it is directional dependent. For shear forces, it is positive for an upward
force, negative for a downward force like gravity. The code can be found in appendix A.2, line
128-154.
4.2.3.2
Cable contact force
Except for the situation with straight cables, every single system of post tensioned concrete
beams have contact forces. When these are not point loads, as described in 4.2.3.1, they are
added as distributed loads.
From the theory, see section 2.1, the domain loads from the tension curve can be split into
vertical, equation 4.13 and a horizontal distributed loads, equation 4.14.
27
==PAGE:40==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
pv(x) = P(x) ·
1
R(x) · cos(ϕ(x))
(4.13)
ph(x) = P(x) ·
1
R(x) · sin(ϕ(x))
(4.14)
Where P(x) is the tension force in the curve at the point, ϕ(x) is the angle of the curve to
horizontal at the point, and R(x) is the radius of curvature to the point. Depending on the
curve segments, these have the possibility of being constant, or vary by the value of x. All these
have the possibility of being dependent of the location looked at.
The force in the tension curve, P(x), changes based on friction between the steel tension cable
and the tubing it runs through, added into the program in section 4.3. It also varies over the
length based on anchorage loss, and other losses not modeled in this program, relaxation, creep
and shrinkage. R(x) is constant with curve segments which are made of circular arcs. With
cubic functions on the other had, they vary by distance. The curve radius can also be written
as
1
R = κ
⇔
R = 1
κ
(4.15)
From equation 4.15, it is seen that instead of using the radius of curvature, the curvature can
be used instead. The last part needed to calculate the contact forces is the angle, which also
varies over a distance. In this case, the angle is calculated for every element node, see section
4.2.2.2.
The program only accepts uniform domain loads over an elements. A simple supported beam is
by default split into 8 elements, where a three support system is 24, illustrated in section 4.1.2,
ﬁgure 4.5. To apply the loads, some approximations have to be made. The curvature κ(x) is
averaged from a number of points, see 4.2.2.3, to get one value per element. Both P(x) and ϕ(x)
are values calculated for each node, and are therefore averaged between the two values to get a
pv and a ph which acts over each element. Once calculated, each distributed load is added in
a matrix, dL, which accepts domain load values as rows, dL(i,:)=[el, local DOF, size], changed
from the original script in section 4.2.1.2. Apart from pv and ph, there are two other domain
loads which are added to the beam. Self-weight is added, as well as a vertical load by the size
-pl. If the user wants to add a distributed load over an element, or the entire beam, it can be
done by changing pl value, or adding more rows of loads to the matrix. The MATLAB code for
dL can be seen in appendix A.2 line 157-169, while the MATLAB code for pv and ph can be
seen in appendix A.2 line 76-90.
Because of the way the domain load is calculated, it makes the use of linear curve segments
more diﬃcult. With linear curve segments, the curvature is zero, and there are therefore no
distributed load from the curve. If all the curve segments were linear functions, every load
would be applied as nodal forces, see section 4.2.3.1 or section 2.1. It is possible to make the
program like that, but by wanting to make a curve without any kinks, it was not a way to get
there.
28
==PAGE:41==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
4.2.4
Calculating stresses
The stresses in the beam are calculated. In concrete structures, it is preferred that only com-
pression is present in the proﬁle. It is therefore essential to know if the proﬁle is subjected
to tension or compression throughout the cross section. Here, Navier’s formula seen as the
equations 4.17 are used to calculate the stress in the top and bottom of the beam. The stresses
are calculated inside the core function, not in a subfunction. Therefore, it already has access
to the variables containing the sectional forces F1 and M and a calculated moment of inertia.
The distance from the ideal center is found from the top and bottom, zib zit, see equation 4.16
zib =
Ab · h
2 + (Ep
Ec −1) · Ap · ap
Ab + (Ep
Ec −1) · Ap
zit = h −zib
(4.16)
σx = N
A + M
Iy
· zit
σx = N
A + M
Iy
· zib
(4.17)
The stresses in the top and bottom ﬁbers are then plotted for every element and every curve.
The function plotS is used for this, see section 4.4. Figure 4.10 is an illustration of the calculated
stresses taken from the case described in section 5.1.5. The MATLAB code which calculates
the stresses can be seen in appendix A.2 line 243-252.
(a) Stresses in top ﬁbers
(b) Stresses in bottom ﬁbers
Figure 4.10: Plot illustration stresses for the beam element due to prestressing
29
==PAGE:42==
4.2. CORE - FEM CURVECORE.M
4.2.5
Optimization value - Moment sum
The optimizer function fminsearch needs a value to minimize to be able to work. This value
needs to depend on two things. It needs to react to any of the changes in the fminsearch input
variable which gets adjusted, and it needs to change based on any changes in the node values.
The objective of the optimizer which runs through the core function, is to make the most eﬃcient
curve at a given P0 value. This was determined to be based on the moment distribution through
the beam. At any given P0, if the maximum moment in the beam is low, the remaining bearing
capacity is large. At ﬁrst, the minimizer just tried to minimize the maximum value of the
moment. If this maximum was present more than one place, the value might just change in one
of the two points with another adjusted run, and fminsearch would not know the adjustment
actually was better. Therefore, to get the minimizer to react to any changes in the curve, it
was better to sum every point in the moment matrix M. Because of the possibility of negative
moments, which are just as bad statically, it is the absolute value of every point which needs to
be summed.
Instead of just adding every point together, it was thought that a better solution was to calculate
the area under the moment graph and use that as the minimizer value. This seemed like a better
choice, since it weighted the values based on distance between each point, instead of just the
value. After implementation, it was observed that even though it was reducing the overall area,
it created spikes in the moment compared to the other method, see ﬁgure 4.11. This was then
reverted.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.11: Moment plots. Left point sum, right moment area integral
30
==PAGE:43==
4.3. CABLE FORCE THROUGH LENGTH P(X) - FRICTIONMODULE.M
4.3
Cable force through length P(x) - frictionModule.m
The post tension cable force is not constant throughout the beam in practise. Two of the factors
which reduce the cable force are friction loss and anchorage loss. These two have been modeled
in the program in frictionModule.m and is described in the following sections.
4.3.1
Friction loss
The friction loss calculations in the program are based on equation 2.6, seen below as 4.18.
P = P0 · e−(µ·ϕ+k·x)
(4.18)
To create the calculation, three diﬀerent situations could occur in the curve which needs to be
calculated correctly. In the end, the friction loss calculation was evaluated in section 5.2.
The three situations are:
• A post tension cable curve with no inﬂection points.
• A post tension cable curve with inﬂection points in element nodes.
• A post tension cable curve with inﬂection points between element nodes.
To satisfy the ﬁrst situation, calculating the cable force using equation 2.6 in every element
node gives the same results as analytical calculations for ever element node.
To satisfy the second situation, a check needs to be performed at every node to see if an inﬂection
point in in the node. If there is, P0 needs to be set to the value of P(x) in the node. The angle
change needs to be based on the angle of the curve in the inﬂection point as well, since the
angle change (curvature) after an inﬂection point is positive instead of negative, or the other
way around. Instead of doing a check every time, it is easier to just calculate the friction loss
over an element one at a time, by setting the angle, and P0 node to node. This was conﬁrmed
to be exactly the same as using the change between the start angle and the angle at the node
and P0 = Pjack to calculate every point for the ﬁrst situation.
To satisfy the last situation where an inﬂection point is between two element nodes, a calcula-
tion was made checking each curve segment for placements of possible inﬂection points. If an
inﬂection point of a curve segment was not in an element node the calculation of P(x) for the
two nodes of the element was changed a bit. First, P(x) is calculated in the ﬁrst node, then
set as P0 together with the angle, then the angle change was calculated to the inﬂection point,
31
==PAGE:44==
4.3. CABLE FORCE THROUGH LENGTH P(X) - FRICTIONMODULE.M
and a new P0 was set at the inﬂection point. The rest of the loss is then calculated to the next
node, and P(x) was saved in this point.
By doing the steps above an accurate value of P(x) is found for every element node. It does lead
to slight calculation errors, though, because if the inﬂection point is in-between two element
nodes, the only saved values are in the nodes. Therefore, ﬁnding the average between the two
might be slightly higher or lower, since drawing a line between two values of P(x) might result
in a bit smaller or a bit larger area than an analytical calculation.
The friction loss is calculated in steps. First, it is calculated as if the beam is jacked in one
side. Then, the friction is calculated as if it is jacked in the other end. These are saved in PXst
and PXsl respectively, with each row being the P(x) value at the start of an element and the
end of an element.
If the beam is jacked from both ends, which is used as default in this program, the maximum
value of PXst and PXsl at every element node is found. This procedure is illustrated in 4.12,
where the blue line represents the friction calculated from the start of the beam and the red
line from the end of the beam. The solid lines are the saved values for P(x) after friction loss.
Figure 4.12: Illustraion of the calculated cable force after applying friction
The MATLAB code for the friction loss calculates in frictionModule can be seen in appendix
A.8.
4.3.2
Anchorage loss
Anchorage loss is like friction loss just in the opposite direction. Friction is because the jacking
force is pulling the tension cable towards the anchor and increasing its length. This rubs against
the cable tubing, which through friction reduces the force applied in the cable due to jacking.
Once the jacking is done, it needs to be anchored to the end of the beam. For the cable to get
locked into the anchor it needs to give a few millimeters. This slacks a bit on the cable creating
a cascading eﬀect of the cable ever so slightly moving into the beam.
Anchor loss is illustrated together with the loss due to friction and the jacking force in ﬁgure
4.13.
32
==PAGE:45==
4.3. CABLE FORCE THROUGH LENGTH P(X) - FRICTIONMODULE.M
Figure 4.13: Cable force P(x): red is friction loss, blue is anchorage loss, black is jacking force
The implementation of anchor loss in the program is based on the theory described in section
2.3. Instead of ﬁnding lsl from equation 2.14, the total draw-in loss area from equation 2.12 is
found instead. This area is depicted on ﬁgure 4.13 as the area between the P(x) after anchor
loss, the red line, and the P(x) after only applying friction loss, the blue line. This is then
compared to the total area from the start to x0, (x0 · (P0 −P(x0)) −Africloss) · 2. The area
Africloss is the area between a line of y = P0 , seen in ﬁgure 4.13 in black and the friction graph
P(x), seen in ﬁgure 4.13 as red. This is illustrated on ﬁgure 4.14, showing how the program
calculates the area, then if the area is not big enough, calculates the area to the next node.
Figure 4.14: Illustration of the program steps for calculating anchorage loss
In ﬁgure 4.14, for each node the area above the friction curve (the hatched area) is found and
the corresponding square area marked as a dark gray box. It is then compared to the area found
from equation 2.12, indicated as the thick black line. If the calculated area is equal or larger
than the total area of the anchorage loss it stops. Otherwise, it continues and the anchor loss
area is calculated for every node until the required area is less than or equal to the calculated
anchorage loss.
33
==PAGE:46==
4.4. PLOTTING
When the area is found to be greater than EpAps, the new value of P(x) is saved in each element
node as
Pnew(xn) = P(xn) −2(P(xn) −P(x0))
(4.19)
Where xn is the distance x of the node, and x0 is the x distance to the node where the inequality
Anchorloss≥EpAps is satisﬁed.
The disadvantage of calculating this only in every node is
described in the evaluation section 5.3. The MATLAB code for the calculation of the anchorage
loss can be found in the frictionModule in appendix A.8 as a sub function, line 97-175.
4.4
Plotting
The functions which draw the diﬀerent plots in the program are split into individual ﬁles. This
it to make them available for the user to call independently of each other, and any of the
calculation functions. A function plotAll.m was was created to call all the plots which a user
might want to know.
Three of the plot functions are reused from BygFEM ramme.
These
are plotS, plotTop, and plotDof. The function plotS has been changed slightly to update the
graph titles, and all the function comments have been translated to english in all three original
plotters.
4.4.1
plotAll.m
The function plotAll receives all the input it needs to call all four plot functions.
It calls
plotCurve, plotDof, and plotTop ﬁrst, then six calls of plotS. Each call of plotS plots a diﬀerent
sectional force, or other system property. It is because of plotS that many of the variables are
saved as rows of elements with two columns. The value in the ﬁrst column is the value in the
ﬁrst node of an element and the second is the value in the last column of the element. By doing
this, plotS was used to plot the stress, as well as the force in the cable, P(x) which is saved as
the variable PXanchor when applying the anchor loss to the beam.
4.4.2
Plotting the curve - plotCurve.m
Based on all the curve data calculated in the core function, a function which plots the tension
curve through beam is generated. This is to give the user a quick visual representation of the
curve solution, instead of having to pace the points into another program. In addition to the
tension curve, the beam height is showed in the same plot. This is done to show where the
curve is in relation to the top or bottom of the beam, which, if deﬁned, is the tension curve
boundary. The curve plot is created with the function plotCurve. To plot the curve, the function
34
==PAGE:47==
4.5. CURVEFITTING - CURVESIGMA.M
coeﬃcients {a,b,c,d} are loaded together with the element node X values, which were deﬁned
in 4.1.2. From the coeﬃcients and the X values, the height of the curve is then calculated from
the function of the cubic equation: f(x) = a · x3 + b · x2 + c · x + d. A number of values between
each element node are also calculated, to make the curve plot smooth. This is required since
every line drawn in the plot is a straight line. Each curve segment has to be plotted one by one,
until the complete curve proﬁle is drawn. The beam height boundaries are drawn afterwards.
An example can be seen in ﬁgure 4.15. The MATLAB code can be seen in appendix A.4.
Figure 4.15: Plot illustrating tension curve and beam boundaries
4.5
Curveﬁtting - curveSigma.m
The core function calculates all the section forces to a given curve with a given P0 value. To
utilize the jacking force P0 to its maximum, the curve proﬁle can be adjusted to apply greater
or lesser force to the beam, depending on the load situation and beam geometry. To do this,
the minimizer function fminsearch is used. It is a Nelder-Mead simplex, seen in section 2.4. It
uses an input variable, runs a function and receives a function value. For this program, it uses
the input variable YKg=[y1, h1, y2, h2, ..., yncu, hncu] to adjust, and receives the optimizer value
based on the moment, described in section 4.2.5.
A single run of fminsearch, starting with the curve as a straight line, will optimize the curve so
the moment in the beam is as small as possible. It starts by running the core function, then
running the core function again with an adjustment in one of the values of YKg. Each time it
runs through the core function, it has optimized the curve. It is possible for it to adjust the
input variable to run for solutions that are worse than one it already has found, but it will
discard them if they are not improvements in the optimizer value. One run of fminsearch on a
simple supported beam with length 10 m, height 0.6 m and a uniform distributed load modelled
in MATLAB is shown in ﬁgure 4.16.
35
==PAGE:48==
4.5. CURVEFITTING - CURVESIGMA.M
(a) Number of iterations
(b) Curve for number of iterations
Figure 4.16: Illustrations of one completed fminsearch
In ﬁgure 4.16a we see the optimization value decrease very fast, and then after iteration ∼30,
the optimizer does not improve that much after that. After iteration ∼150, the change in core
function value is even smaller, it is nearly constant. Figure 4.16b shows the curve height in the
beam elements to a number of iterations. In the depicted curve, there are only 6 values which
fminsearch varies. These are the height and slope in each beam end and the height and slope
of the curve in the middle of the beam . Because of boundaries placed on the fminsearch input
variable, explained in more detail in section 4.5.1, the possible variable values are limited, and
three of them are even locked to a ﬁxed value. The ﬁxed values are the height of the curve at
the ends, which is set to 0, and the slope of the middle point, which is 0.
It is seen in 4.16b that the curve starts as a straight line, quickly testing if moving the middle
point up or down is the correct choice, and then steadily moving the middle point down, while
changing the start and end slopes. The last adjustment to the curve parameters is the slope in
the beginning being higher than the one in the end, making the asymmetric. When a result like
this appeared after an fminsearch, the situation was run again from the straight line starting
position on the same machine. This resulted in the exact same adjustments being made in the
same iteration. This means it follows a set path based on initial guess and the function it is
minimizing.
To try and get an even more optimized solution than after that one run, fminsearch was run
again, this time starting at the best solution presented by the previous fminsearch. By doing
so, a solution with a smaller optimization value was found. This can be continued, running an
fminsearch again and again, starting from the last known best solution. For the beam from
ﬁgure 4.16b, an fminsearch sequence is depicted in ﬁgure 4.17
36
==PAGE:49==
4.5. CURVEFITTING - CURVESIGMA.M
Figure 4.17: Minimizer value and iteration count for a sequence of fminsearch runs
The method of restarting fminsearch from the last known minimized solution, to get a solution
with an even smaller optimizer value increases the runtime of the program a lot. The reason why
the solution gets better with more runs of fminsearch, is because of the way the Nelder–Mead
simplex method works, explained in detail in section 2.4. After a lot of function calls, the simplex
gets so small, that it has diﬃculty ﬁnding a better solution. Restarting the fminsearch function,
regenerates the simplex12345, and it is able to continue.
It is also known, that fminsearch
actually is one of the slower minimizers, as well as being weak at handling functions with many
variables, like the situation of the curve optimizer.
It is also possible that a minimizer runs stop, or become constant because the minimizer hits a
local minimum. To remove the local minimums, a lot of tests have been performed to try make
sure they don’t exist. It does seem like, if it is local minimums it ﬁnds, that these minimums
are very shallow, and by regenerating the simplex it is possible for the optimizer to get out of
it.
Based on the optimizer values shown in ﬁgure 4.17, ﬁgure 4.18 shows the values found after each
fminsearch minimizer has been completed. Figure 4.18a illustrates the number of completed
fminsearch runs and the corresponding optimizer value.
Figure 4.18b shows the optimized
curvature of the tendon, for each completed run of fminsearch.

3. http://se.mathworks.com/MATLABcentral/ﬁleexchange/33328-improving-the-convergence-of-nelder-mead–
and-so-fminsearch-
4. https://se.mathworks.com/MATLABcentral/newsreader/view thread/96684
5. https://arxiv.org/pdf/1104.5183.pdf - see page 6
