==PAGE:50==
4.5. CURVEFITTING - CURVESIGMA.M
(a) Number of fminsearch runs calls
(b) Curve for number of iterations
Figure 4.18: Curve and minimizer value after each completed fminsearch
The MATLAB code for the curve ﬁtter function curveSigma can be seen in appendix A.10.
4.5.1
Boundaries
In MATLAB, the function fminsearch does not include the ability to create boundaries. To be
able to add boundaries to fminsearch, a user generated function, fminsearchbnd, is run instead.
This gives the ability to add upper bounds and lower bounds to the input variable parameters
of fminsearch. As an example, if it wants to ﬁnd the smallest value of x2, with an upper bound
of -1 and a lower bound of -3, the variable can still start out of bounds, and will be, on the
ﬁrst iteration adjusted to be within bounds. It will then use the simplex to move towards the
smallest value of x2 while staying within bounds. In this case it will land on -1, even though
there are possible better solutions out of bounds.
This is useful in regards to the curve information presented in YKg. Here, the heights, which
are every odd numbered element of YKg, need to stay within the beam dimensions in most
cases. The upper and lower limits can also be used to ﬁx one of the elements of YKg to a value.
By doing so, it decreases the runtime of the program, because it then has fewer variables to
change to ﬁnd the minimum. As an example, limiting three of the six variables on a simply
supported beam, it just has three it can change to get the minimum optimizer value.
Regarding the bounds in the curve optimizer, every odd number in YKg, the heights, should be
upper bound to at most h
2, and lower bound to at least −h
2. A cover layer should be taken into
account as well. This ensures that the curve it creates by adjusting the input variable is within
the proﬁle boundary. Without this limit, it could ﬁnd optimal solutions where the curve was
way out of bounds, or where the curve disregarded the middle support and just eliminated all
moments entirely.
38
==PAGE:51==
4.6. OPTIMIZING THE JACKING FORCE - MINSIGMA.M
Suggestions for values to ﬁx, to decrease run time:
• Start and end height of the tension curve to 0, if the beam is not clamped at the ends.
• Slope of the tension curve over additional vertical supports in multi-span beams to 0.
• Slope in the middle node to 0, if the static system is mirrored.
The MATLAB code to create the bounds in the curve ﬁtter function curveSigma can be seen
in appendix A.10 line 23-48.
4.6
Optimizing the jacking force - minSigma.m
The curve is optimized for a static value of the prestressing force P0 in section 4.5. Another
layer has been created around the curve minimizer, which changes the value of P0.
The post tension forces, both the node loads, bL and the domain loads, dL, are directly pro-
portional with the jacking force P0. On the same curve proﬁle, increasing P0 increases the
force contribution to the beam, and decreasing the force, P0, decreases the forces applied to the
beam. Thereby, P0 cannot be changed without changing all the variables in YKg, the vector
which makes up the tension curve proﬁle if optimizing for curve eﬃciency.
The goal of adjusting P0 is to create the best ﬁtting curve to a situation while reducing the
overall steel needed to create the curve. The added steel volume introduced by changing the
curve length is small compared to changing the cross section area of the steel needed throughout
the entire span by increasing P0.
To achieve this, another fminsearch function is created. It gets an input variable of the value
of the jacking force, P0, and then varies this jacking force until its optimizer value is as low
as possible. The chosen optimizer value for this fminsearch, is based on the stresses in the
beam, namely the top and bottom ﬁber stresses. For concrete beams, a negative stress results
in tension forces in the concrete, and the tension yield capacity of concrete is very low compared
to the compression strength. Therefore, the optimizer ﬁnds the lowest tension force in the top
and in the bottom of the beam, takes the minimum of those two, and takes the numerical value
of this. By taking the numerical value, the minimizer will try to get it as low as possible, thereby
adjusting the input variable until the optimizer value reaches 0. This limit can be changed by
adding or subtracting from the minimum stress before the numerical value is taken, to make
this fminsearch optimize the situation for another stress value. A diﬀerence to take note is that
the jacking force optimizer only checks for the minimum stress value and not the entire sum
of stress values, since the inner curve optimizer already changes the entire curve based on the
moments.
39
==PAGE:52==
4.6. OPTIMIZING THE JACKING FORCE - MINSIGMA.M
For every iteration of the outer fminsearch, the inner fminsearch is run as described in section
4.5. After each iteration, the best value found for the input variable in the curve ﬁtter, YKg, is
saved as a global variable to be used as the start guess in the next iteration. This reduces the
runtime by eliminating the ﬁrst number of adjustments done to the curve parameters compared
to if it started as a straight centric line every time.
Figure 4.19 shows two graphs. The ﬁrst graph, ﬁgure 4.19a, illustrates the optimization value
of the outer fminsearch per iteration, the minimum stress per P0 adjustment. It can be seen
that the optimization value is decreasing with the number of iterations. There are peaks and
valleys. This is because it shows the minimum stress for every single adjustment of P0, and not
just for every adjustment which results in a better minimization value. The right graph, ﬁgure
4.19b, illustrates the value of the post tension jacking force P0 to the corresponding adjustment
number of fminsearch. Common for both the illustrations, is that the optimization value is close
to zero after 30 runs of the fminsearch function.
(a) Number of input variable adjustments and cor-
responding numerical minimum stress
(b) Value of P0 for each adjustment
Figure 4.19: Values of a single run of the outer fminsearch for the jacking force optimizer
There seems to be no signiﬁcant changes in the optimization value and the P0 from iteration
30 until the end. It has been tried to force fminserach to stop earlier, so it would stop after
the 30 iterations, by changing the options in fminsearch. This is done by changing TolX to
make it stop if it does not adjust P0 by more than 1000N. It is also needed to change TolFun,
which stops the minimizer if the minimization value is not able to be changed by more than
5000Pa. This is because fminsearch does not stop unless both criteria are reached. Currently
though, fminsearch adjusts the input variable by smaller amounts than that, even if they are
set. It would decrease the total runtime of the program, if the number of fminsearch calls were
reduced. The MATLAB code for the jacking force optimizer function minSigma can be seen in
appendix A.9.
40
==PAGE:53==
4.6. OPTIMIZING THE JACKING FORCE - MINSIGMA.M
4.6.1
Boundaries
For this fminsearch, fminseachbnd has been used instead. This is to create the lower bound of
P0 = 10000. This bound was created to try and keep fminsearch from adjusting P0 to a value
less than 0, due to a limitation in the friction module. Here, the program stops and gives an
error if a negative P0 value is run through the friction module.
This can still happen though because fminsearchbnd does not prevent fminsearch from adjusting
the input variable out of the bounds and run, it just tells fminsearch that the result it gets from
this is invalid. If it adjusts P0 this low, it is because the start value of P0 in the input variable
is too high, and it began adjusting the value of the input variable in large steps, passing zero.
The start value of the input variable should then be dramatically lowered manually.

5.
Evaluations
The following sections, section 5.1, section 5.2, and section 5.3, are about evaluating the accuracy
of the program compared to analytical calculations of the same cases. These cases are smaller
beams which can be quickly calculated analytically. They are tests of parts of the script, and
not of the full program in every case. The cases were evaluated regularly, with every major
change in the core function. The cases described below have been created in the ﬁnal program,
testing speciﬁc parts of it.
5.1
Evaluation of section forces
In the following chapter, a number of simple cases have been establish in the program and
compared to analytical calculations from CAS, to evaluate the output of the program and its
accuracy. A jacking force P0 was chosen and a tension curve is modelled in the program. It is
possible to ﬁnd the normal force, shear force and moment distribution for the structural system,
which is the forces that are compared.
For every case in this section, a 10 meter long beam, with a constant cross-section with height
0.6m and width 0.3 m has been modeled. Furthermore, no external loads are applied to the
situation, only the forces from the tension curve, no dead load. The jacking force P0 is 2000kN.
Friction and anchor loss are not applied in these cases, the curve has a constant force of
P(x) = P0 = 2000kN.

5.2
Validation of friction
A validation is carried out to evaluate to output of frictionModule. Here an analytical calculation
is carried out for comparison of the output. From section 4.3.1, the friction was calculated from
both ends (PXst and PXsl). For a beam jacked from both ends the maximum value of each end
in PXst and PXsl was saved. To evaluate the friction calculation in the program it is enough
to look at the jacking force P(0) = P0 and the friction value at the end of the beam. The value
at the end of the beam depends on the value calculated throughout the nodes. If this value is
matches in the end, it is correct for every value calculated.
For each of the cases evaluated below the value at the opposite end of the jacked end is the
same if it was jacked from the left, PXst, and from the right, PXsl. Therefore, it is only needed
to compare PXst to an analytical calculation of the curve jacked from the left. Three diﬀerent
cases have been created to evaluate P(x). Case 1, section 5.2.1, has a quadratic curve, where
the friction is nearly linear. Case 2, section 5.2.2, has one cubic curve with an inﬂection in
an element node. Case 3, section 5.2.3, is a cubic function, with an inﬂection point not in an
element node.
The curves have been modeled in the program only with the curve in mind, not taking into
account beam dimensions. As described in friction theory 2.2, the curve length is assumed to
be equal to the beam length in the program. The same assumption has been made for the
analytical calculation. However, a calculation assuming the actual length of the curve has been
performed as well.

To evaluate if the anchorage loss gets calculated as described in section 4.3.2, an evaluation of
the exact same beam system and tension curve proﬁle is made. From the description of the
application of the anchorage loss in section 4.3.2, it ﬁnds the closest node where the anchorage
loss is equal or greater than the analytical anchorage loss. Thereby, changing the placement
of the nodes, it might be able to ﬁnd a node which would reduce the deviation between the
program calculations and the analytical calculations.
The element node placements are dependent on the curve nodes as well as the placement of
the supports. Without changing the support system or the curve, a way to change the node
placements are by adding more points between each curve node. This is done by increasing the
nelPrNcu variable. A default it is 4, but for this evaluation it was changed, to evaluate the
system with values of nelPrNcu between 4 and 9.
Figure 5.13 shows the percentage distance between curve node 2, x = 2.5 and curve node
3, x = 3.97. The vertical black line indicates the analytically calculated position where the
anchorage loss meets the friction loss. The points indicate the placement of the element nodes,
where the number of element divisions are indicated on the y-axis.

5.3. EVALUATION OF ANCHORAGE
Figure 5.13: Variation of nelPrNcu for a curve segment
Table 5.14 shows the accuracy of the anchorage calculations in the program compared to the
analytical calculations. It can be seen in the table, that the element distribution which gives the
lowest error ratio in the anchorage loss is by using 7 elements per curve segment in this case. It
also shows that just by adding more points, does not by default decrease the error ratio. This
is also seen in the tables D.2-D.3 in the appendix. This can be seen on ﬁgure 5.13, that it runs
the anchorage loss to the closest point to the right of the black line. As a consequence of the
setup of frictionModule, if the anchorage loss calculated at an element node is smaller than the
required area of anchorage loss, it is increased by calculating the anchorage loss from the next
node. Therefore, even if a point is close to the black line, if it is not on the right side, it just
runs to the next point, which is the closest to the black line. In the case of nelPrNcu = 9, this
shows that the distance to the closest point is greater than or equal to all the other values of
nelPrNcu apart from 4. This is also represented in the table, with the greatest error ratio. A
subdivition of 7 elements per node segment is the best choice, for this curve. This cannot be set
as default however, because every curve is diﬀerent. In Appendix D the anchorage calculations
can be seen from the analytical calculation and the ones found using MATLAB.

6.
Examples of over constrained optimiza-
tions
In the following sections, a number of statically indeterminate systems are run through the
program to show how it works in diﬀerent situations. Each system has been run through the
post tension cable force optimizer ﬁrst. The results of each of these runs are shown, among
them a number of graphs. The ﬁgures for each case show the curve proﬁle, the stress diagrams
for the stress in the top and bottom, the moment and shear distribution, the normal force and
the cable force graph.
Next, each system is run through just the curve optimizer, setting the jacking force at each end
to 120% of the force found in the other optimizer, again showing ﬁgures for the new section
forces and curve proﬁle.
In each case, the curve nodes are bound, so that they are never closer than 25cm from the top
or the bottom of the beam, no matter what the height of the beam is set in the individual cases.
All the cases, except the two clamped systems have the curve ﬁxed to the center in the ends.
This is purely to improve runtime, since it would ﬁnd that this is the best height for the curve
even without the bounds if there is a hinge support at the ends.
Furthermore, the MATLAB input and output for each run can be found in appendix E together
with a larger illustration of the optimized post tension curves. The run times indicated in the
text for each case indicate how long the program took to complete. The run times are computer
speciﬁc and depend on hardware and CPU allocated to the program. They are there to indicate
if the optimizer takes a long time or is quick.
55
==PAGE:68==

==PAGE:69==
6.1. 3-SPAN BEAM

Figure 6.3: MATLAB output data for the jacking force optimized 3-span beam with a distributed load
57
==PAGE:70==
6.1. 3-SPAN BEAM
It found the optimal curve segments to have the height and slope in each curve node as table
6.1 and 6.2 rounded to nearest millimeter.
The two points on the beam which were found to have stress close to zero, which made the
optimizer stop, were above the vertical supports at a
3 and 2a
3 . The optimizer also showed that
slacking the cable causes a few things to occured. Firstly, the curve optimizer was unable to
create a curve with enough curvature to counteract the vertical loads from the self-weight and
the external loads. That did not stop it from trying, and it is evident that where ever possible,
the curve is out of the bounds set for the curve nodes. This is due to the way the bounding
works. It is able to create any curve it needs, but the curve nodes need to be inside the bounds.
This can be seen in three places. In the left and the right span, the curve is lower than the set
bound until it hits the curve node at x = a
6 and x = 5a
6 respectively. Around this point, the
curvature is downward, as seen in ﬁgure 6.3d, where the slope of the shear turns form positive
to negative.
In the middle span, one would imagine that keeping a positive curvature would be ideal to
minimize the moment.
However, the optimizer ﬁnds that having the curve slightly out of
bounds and then have a slight downward curvature at the middle of the side spans is best. This
creates three stationary points in the curve in this span.
It also ﬁnds that above each support, creating a curve which acts like a quartic function with
four inﬂection points spanning the three curve nodes is a better ﬁt. It chooses this solution
because if the curve does not need to hit the curve node at the support, but instead the curve
node just before it, it can increase the curvature of the rest of the curve proﬁle. The curve node
over the support is bound to a set height which it cannot exceed. The cable in-between the
nodes can though.
6.1.2
3-span beam - Curveﬁtter.m run
A curve optimizer was run for the system as well. This was done by using the exact same
functions FEM CurveCore, setElementData, frictionModule, and curveSigma as the jacking
force optimizer run before it without changing any boundaries or loads. This time the curve
was optimized for a single value of the jacking force P0 instead of the one which would give zero
stress in the top or the bottom. The jacking force P0 was set to 120% of the one found in the
jacking force optimizer. In this case, it was set to ≈12.7MN.

The curve-ﬁtter Curveﬁtter.m with the new P0 took just under 25 minutes to run, creating
the curve from a straight cable in the center to the one shown in ﬁgure 6.4 by restarting
fminsearchbnd 37 times, as described in section 4.5. Compared to the curve found in the jacking
force optimizer and the corresponding section force plots, it can be seen that increasing P0 by
20% creates a curve which has fewer inﬂection points. By looking at the shear and moment
curves, it is evident that the output curve is able to counteract the aﬀects of the self-weight and
the external load nearly completely.
Looking at the values for the height of the curve at the element nodes, YK, seen in appendix
E.1 it shows that the ﬁrst and last curve segment is still slightly below the boundary height of
the curve nodes. The tension cable is about 25mm, and increasing the jacking force P0 a bit
more and adjust the boundary height could prevent this. The span is still a bit too long for
the post tension cable with this jacking force to perfectly counteract the loads on the beam.
Increasing the beam height, to increase the possible curvature of the curve, or increasing P0 will
counteract it even better.

2-span asymmetric load - P0Optimizer run

beam was run through the post tension cable force optimizer, minSigma. To apply the dis-
tributed load, dL was changed from the default program. The default application of pl was
moved out of the for loop and another loop was created to apply pl on just the ﬁrst half of the
elements, see appendix E.3. Nothing was changed in bL compared the to the default program.

output can be found in appendix E.3.

6.4. 2-SPAN UNEQUAL DISTANCE
Unlike the previous cases, the minimum stress was found in the bottom ﬁbers, in the long right
span. By comparing the moment curve and the stress curve, it is evident that the stress is
actually negative between two element nodes. Since the stresses are calculated in the element
nodes, it is not able to correctly identify the negative stress. This can be prevented by changing
the minimizer to track for 1 MPa instead of 0 MPa, or by increasing the number of element per
curve segment.

6.5. 2-SPAN POINT LOAD
6.5
2-span point load
The ﬁfth case is a two span beam with a point load right between the left support and the
middle support. With a length of a = 80m, width of b = 1m and height of h = 2m, it has a pin
support at the left end, and vertical supports at u3 = a
2, and u4 = a. The vertical point load
force is pl = 1000 · 103N. Unlike all the other cases, no self-weight was added to this beam.
This was to show how the curve would be calculated with just a single point load in the system.
Figure 6.21 shows the system.
Figure 6.21: 2-span beam system with a point load
6.5.1
2-span point load - P0Optimizer.m run
With the beam dimensions, support placements and distributed load as described above, the
beam was run through the post tension cable force optimizer, minSigma. There were a couple
lines of code changed compared to the default program. In dL, the distributed load pl line was
removed, as well as the self-weight application. The cable forces pv and ph placement in dL was
changed to match, so there were no rows of zeroes in dL. To apply the load, a row was added to
the bottom of bL with the global node and the force size, bL(size(bL, 1)+1, :) = [D(4, 5) −pl·10].
In the code, pl = 100e3 like many of the other cases, which gives the value of the point load as
described in above in section 6.5. The changed code in bL and dL can be seen in appendix E.5.
The output of the optimized tension curve is displayed in the ﬁgure 6.22. Furthermore, the data
output can be found in appendix E.5.

The curve optimizer took a little over 2 minutes to run this system. With the increased cable
force, the cable is inside the boundary in every element node. Calculating the curve height
between element nodes from ABCD might show it being slightly outside the boundary. This
is an issue which persists with FEM programs, sometimes the element divisions are not ﬁne
enough to catch every detail.
The moment and shear graphs, ﬁgure 6.25c and ﬁgure 6.25d, show that the point load gets
negated by building up vertical force from the curve by making the curve as straight as possible
to the point load, and then turn as fast as possible to force as much of the force as possible
into that point. If there were added extra curve nodes around the point load point as there are
around the supports, it would possibly be even more pronounced forming a V.

7.
Discussion
7.1
fminsearch
In a program like this where one variable is dependent on another facilitate solutions which
increase the completion time. This is the case when trying to ﬁnd the lowest possible value of
the jacking force still able to match the stress criteria of the beam. When using the functions
fminsearch and fminsearchbnd they can only adjust one function variable at a time, though
this could be a vector. The minimizer value on the other hand can only be a single value.
Therefore, when fminsearch is used as the optimization function, an fminsearch needs to run
on an fminsearch to optimize both the curve height and the jacking force. By utilizing this
method, the run time of the program is increasing exponentially. Having to optimize on even
more variables, would increase the run time even further and make the program unsuitable for
quick calculations of a post tension curve.
A number of parameters have an eﬀect on the run time as well. The function fminsearch has
built-in optimization options. It is possible to increase the tolerance on the function value and
the tolerance of the optimized value X, both positive scalars. The default for both stopping
criteria is set to 1e-4. Dependent on what fminsearch is optimizing on, and the magnitude of
the minimizer value, changing these value can help decrease the run time, by making it stop
when the function is minimized enough. Making these stopping criteria too small can have an
impact on the program as well. The minimizer fminsearch might sneak into local valleys in
the function it is trying to minimize. This is possible when the simplex gets too small, or it
might just never ﬁnd a way forward, even if there is a tiny change in the minimizer value with
a lower function value. If the stopping criteria are too large, it might stop before it ﬁnds a path
to optimize on. fminsearh distinguishes from other minimizer. If only one of the tolerances
criterion is reached, the function continuous until both tolerances has reached their criteria.
In general, a system with few variables are easier and faster to solve, than a system with many.
The same thing is valid for fminsearch, when trying the optimize the object function. Additional
losses like relaxation, fatigue and creep could be accommodated in the program as well, but
would result in a more complex function. This would increasing the number of variables and
calculations for fminsearch to optimize on in the object function. Here, the algorithm would
take even more variables into account when trying to ﬁnd a minimum. The amount of variables
87
==PAGE:100==
7.2. CABLE CURVE
fminsearch is handling and the complexity of its object function, is also the reason fminsearch is
coming to an end before it reaches a stop criteria. When the minimizer stops, it is possible that
it becomes constant or hits a local minimum in the simplex. However, by restart the optimizer
it is possible to get past this point and ﬁnd a even better function value. This is not the most
programmer friendly way to solve the problem, since it should be possible to ﬁnd a minimum
by just initializing one run of fminsearch. Alternative optimization functions could have been
used to solve the problem in a better way than fminsearch, and could be investigated as possible
improvements. It could be possible it is a problem with the way fminsearch is programmed in
MATLAB which makes the simplex degenerate over many adjustments of the variable.
7.2
Cable Curve
It is possible to establish boundaries for the input vector in fminsearch by using fminsearchbnd.
These boundaries are used to ﬁt the height of the tension curve and limit the jacking force.
However, this can only be done on the values which are directly input and changed by fminsearch.
The curve is therefore only bound in the curve nodes and not over the entire length of the curve
proﬁle. As a consequence, it is possible for the curve height in element nodes to breach these
boundaries, like illustrated in ﬁgure 7.1.
Figure 7.1: Tension curve exceeding boundaries of beam geometry
It is seen that the curve bents down at a bound point just to continue further up after the point.
The post tensioned cable would contribute with a high vertical domain load to the beam at
such points. By increasing the jacking force a tension curve with lower curvature is obtained.
Thus, increasing the jacking force after the ﬁnalisation of the optimization, the tension curve
going out of bounds could be prevented. Furthermore, only the center line of the cable has been
taken into account and not the whole diameter of the cable. In practice the cable would be
interacting with the ducting and the beam at the top or bottom of the ducting, which has not
been taken account. In practice larger displacement of the post tension curve, together with a
construction error, might result in a diﬀerence between practical application and the sectional
forces calculated in the program.
88
==PAGE:101==
7.3. NODE OPTIMIZING
7.3
Node optimizing
The program has the possibility of increasing the element count per curve segment. This is
however the only direct input the user has at increasing the number of element nodes. It is also
one of the slower ways to do it. Through the evaluations in section 5 and over constrained runs
performed in section 6, it is evident that increasing the number of element nodes, or even curve
nodes might be an improvement on the accuracy of the program.
The way anchorage is calculated in frictionModule, increasing the number of element nodes in
the ﬁrst and last curve segments, or even just for a set distance from the supports could be an
improved solution.
Likewise, there are static systems like the case from section 6.5, where added point loads need
to be optimized for. Increasing the number of curve nodes to ensure that at least a curve node
is at or near to the point load could help, as well as increasing the number of curve segments
near the point load, like currently applied around supports. This is not possible as the current
function setElementData has no interaction with bL.
There is currently no easy way to add an extra element node, or curve node. They can be
appended in setElementData before X or XK get sorted by placement, but without the checker,
see appendix A.1 line 28-54, adding curve nodes too close to each other creates results where the
radius of curvature is too small. It would also break some of the functions in FEM CurveCore,
since it assumes that there are only nelPrNcu number of elements between each curve segment.
7.4
Friction & anchorage loss
An analytical calculation of the friction loss has been done in section 5.2, to evaluate the friction
calculation performed in MATLAB. The evaluation compares the analytically calculated value
and the value calculated in the program at the ﬁxed end of the cable if it was jacked from the
left side.
Even if these two values were identical, which they are with the same approximation of curve
length, the program only calculates the friction loss at the element nodes. Thereby, the total
area of friction loss is still only an approximation, and the accuracy would increase with more
element nodes. This is because the friction is assumed linear between each element node. The
analytical calculations might show the friction loss to continue with the same slope past the
node and then ﬂatten out, and integrating this area of the graph would show a discrepancy
between the friction loss applied in the program and an analytical calculation.
Anchorage loss is directly dependent on the friction loss calculations in this program. Therefore,
it inherits the same inaccuracies as mentioned above. Another inaccuracy is the the error ratio
89
==PAGE:102==
7.4. FRICTION & ANCHORAGE LOSS
explained in section 5.3. Apart from the user choosing the correct number of element nodes to
get the anchor loss just right, other solutions could be implemented.
Changing the way the program chooses which node to calculate the friction loss to, could reduce
this error. Right now, it ensures that the required anchor loss area always be lower than the
anchor loss area calculated in the program. Instead it could check for the node which results in
the smallest numeric deviation between the calculated anchor loss in the node and the required
anchor loss area. This could reduce the total error, but the way it is coded in this program, the
error ratio is always that P(x) is lower than the analytical calculation, and never greater. A
P(x) greater than the corresponding analytically calculated P(x), would result in larger overall
normal force in the beam, increasing the stress throughout. This would lead to possible over
estimation of the stress state from a given curve when trying to ﬁnd P0 resulting in zero stress
in an element like P0Optimizer.m. This could lead to failure if no other safety factors were
in use.
Additionally, if the beam would fail because of calculated compression stresses, an
overcompensation of the anchor loss has a negative eﬀect. The stress due to compression would
be even greater, with the correct compensation of the anchor loss.
Another way to reduce the error rate of the anchorage loss, is to create another algorithm for the
calculation of the anchor loss. Like the one used in this program, it would calculate the anchor
loss based on the friction loss. Instead of comparing the friction loss areas just at the element
nodes, it could try to approximate the exact distance x0 where the anchor loss calculated for
x0 is equal to the induced loss from the draw-in. This could be done when calculating with the
current method, and it reaches two nodes, where it is too small in the ﬁrst node, but too large
in the second node. Testing this the area criteria for a virtual node between the two points, and
again between the two best until the areas diﬀer by a very small margin, would approximate
x0. From this, P(x) after anchor loss could be calculated for the element nodes. This would
decrease the area diﬀerence between the analytical calculation and the program by an amount
shown in 7.2.
Figure 7.2: Possible accuracy gain in anchorage loss calculations. Lower hatch is the loss in the anchor calculation
90
==PAGE:103==
7.5. CURVE DISPLACEMENT
It would not reduce the discrepancy to zero, because the values of P(x) are only saved in every
element node. Since there is no element node in x0, there is still a small are area loss compared
to placing an element node in x0. Placing an element node in x0 cannot be done with the
way the program is set up, because that would require x0 to be calculated before the element
topology is created after the friction calculations. Adding this to the program would increase
the amount of calculations in the program, but it should not increase the total run time by any
signiﬁcant amount.
7.5
Curve displacement
Irregularities are found between the curve segments, seen in ﬁgure 7.3. The ﬁgure illustrates
two curve-ends which shows discontinuity in the curve. The horizontal axis shows the length
in meters and the vertical shows the height in meters as well. It can be seen on the axises that
the leap is in the magnitude of 10−8m. Since the leap is so small, it is not signiﬁcant to overall
calculation of the post tensioned curve and is therefore not taken into account in the program.
Figure 7.3: Displacement of curve
The leap was found when the curve nodes over a support was very close to each other. This
created a curve which jumped more than three centimeters in a curve node, a point where they
should be equal. In this case calculations of the coeﬃcients {a,b,c,d} of the cubic function
for the curve segments between the curve nodes result in numerically large values of these
coeﬃcients. Investigating this, the culprit was the precision of MATLAB. By default, variables
are saved as double precision, meaning that it saves 16 signiﬁcant digits. When one or more of
the coeﬃcients {a,b,c,d} are in the tens of thousands, or hundreds of thousands, and only able
91
==PAGE:104==
7.6. CURVE GENERATOR
to use 16 signiﬁcant digits, having to match the height of the curve to 10−4m or even 10−3m
was apparently too much to ask of matlab.
This can be corrected by using other variable types, like variable-precision ﬂoating-point arith-
metic (vpa). vpa set digits default to 32, Compared to double precision, vpa is much slower
though. To make sure the the coeﬃcients {a,b,c,d} would never reach a point where the pre-
cision would create unaccuracies, a minimum distance between curve nodes was set. It was
evaluated if using vpa for some calculations would be better, but doing up to millions of cal-
culations of ABCD, the run time was weighted higher than precision errors of a magnitude of
10−8m.
7.6
Curve generator
In the early phase of the project diﬃculties were encountered when trying to model the post
tension curve by quadratic curve segments or straight lines, see section 3. A smooth continuous
curve was achieved by implementing of cubic curve segments between curve nodes. In a late
stage of the project, another possible way to create the tension curve proﬁle was observed.
When approximating a curve between points, the greater the power of the polynomial, the
better ﬁtting it will be. On the other hand, outside of the points it ﬁts to, they have a tendency
to oscillate. To avoid this, splines are used in many computer programs to draw a continues line
between points given in {x, y}. They work, just like this program, by drawing curve segments
between each point.
A type of spline which could be used in this program is a spline created by cubic curve segments
between each point. Unlike our curve generator however, the spine would only need {x, y} and
not the slope in the point to draw its curve. It does not need the slope, because the curvature to
both segments interacting in a node share the same curvature in the point, and splines assume
that the segment beyond the start and end nodes have zero curvature. From this assumption,
and the {x, y} value of the curve nodes, it would be able to draw splines for the curve proﬁle.
First, this could decrease the total run time of the program dramatically, if the same number
of curve nodes were used, by instead of adjusting two values per node, fminsearch would just
have to adjust the height. Secondly, this could mean that the number of curve nodes could be
increased, maybe even doubled for any system. Even with the doubling of nodes, the program
would maybe still be faster, since tweaking the slope in each point every time it moves up or
down must swallow a lot of time. It is also possible to adjust the start and end curvature of the
spline, so it does not start with zero curvature, to ﬁt the cable curve better.
This might also be able to ﬁx the the boundary problem described in section 7.2. The splines
have not been tested for this program. Using the remaining time to change a major, work-
ing, part of the program seemed like the wrong way to go in consideration of program speed
optimization.
92
==PAGE:105==
7.7. ADDITIONAL LOSSES
7.7
Additional losses
Friction loss and anchorage loss had been established in the program and discussed. However,
there are other eﬀects to take into account when dealing with post-tensioned concrete. Four
other factors have an aﬀect on the cable force of the tensioned cable in post tensioned beams.
These are relaxation, fatigue of the steel cable, creep and shrinkage in the concrete. Friction is
the largest contributor to stress loss in the tension cable. Many of the other losses also depend
heavily on the type of material, and other external factors like moisture and temperature.
Therefore, it was decided to implement friction loss and anchorage loss before the other loss
contributors. During the last phase of the thesis, it was determined to not include modules for
calculations of other losses.
This has the consequence that the cable force is overestimated compared to a real world sit-
uation. The exclusion of the remaining losses does not diminish the programs abilities as an
optimizer tool and as a sectional force calculator of post tensioned beams.
93
==PAGE:106==
94
==PAGE:107==
8.
Further work
8.1
User friendliness
The code generated in the script is far from perfect. As a programmer, the shortest and simplest
code is always preferred. By simplifying the coding, the amount of errors can be reduced and
the fastest run time can be obtained. Many functions, variable calls, etc. in the ﬁnal script are
rather intricate making it easy for errors to occur and increasing the overall run time of the
optimizer.
In addition to that, many of the functions need many input variables and a give a lot of output
variables. This could be changed, so the variables are saved as sub variables in a ﬁeld array,
and instead of making sure every single variable was input to the function, the entire ﬁeld array
could be input, and the needed variables could be pulled from it. This would improve the visual
clutter of every function call.
Furthermore, to increase user friendliness of the program, a GUI could be created. Creating a
GUI for the program would allow the program to be used by users unfamiliar with the actual
calls of the variables, and the user would not have to edit in the actual code when running the
program for a new beam.
8.2
Program improvements
A few possible improvements to the program were discussed in section 7. This included chang-
ing the way anchorage was calculated, section 4.3.2 as well as changing the curve generator in
the program 7.6. With further work on the project, these possible improvements could be inves-
tigated, and if found beneﬁcial, implemented in the program. Besides these two improvements,
trying to bring down the run time of the individual fminsearch calls without compromising the
optimizers ability to minimize the moment curve is a iterative time consuming process.
95
==PAGE:108==
8.3. FIXED CURVE JACKING FORCE OPTIMIZER
8.3
Fixed curve jacking force optimizer
Like the jacking force optimizer P0Optimizer which is an fminsearch on the curve ﬁtter, a
possible alternate way to use the created functions is by using fminsearch to adjust the jacking
force P0 on a user input curve like the one needed in SingleRun. This could be used to adjust
the jacking force in an already determined curve proﬁle to optimize the cable cross sectional
area of the cables needed in the beam. To create this version, it would need to run on a separate
version of FEM CurveCore, which returns another minimizer value because using the moment
sum to determine the jacking force, which is the current minimizer value in FEM CurveCore,
would increase the jacking force more than needed. Instead, the optimizer value could be the
normalized minimum stress like minSigma receives.
96
==PAGE:109==
9.
Conclusion
This thesis describes a series of design decisions which are at the base of a program.
The
program can calculate the sectional forces of a post tensioned concrete beam. Using numerical
modelling, beam systems can be created, and with given curve parameters, the program can
calculate the normal force, shear force and moment distribution, as well as the stress in the top
and bottom ﬁbers of the concrete beam which results from the post tension cable and other
loads in the system. The program can also be used to optimize the cable curve, either for a
given jacking force, or for a stress criterion. The default stress criterion for the curve optimizer
with a varied jacking force is to ﬁnd the best curve and jacking force which results in zero stress
in an element node.
Based on evaluations of the program in section 5 it can be concluded that the resulting forces
which are output when the program is run, are within reasonable margins to be considered cor-
rect. A number of examples of the diﬀerent optimizers on diﬀerent beam systems are displayed
in section 6. The code used in the program is seen in appendix A.
During the development of the program in this thesis, a number of iterations have been made.
The program started by using quadratic curve segments to create the path of the tension curve.
Through tests of the early optimizers, quadratic curve segments as well as linear curve segments
were found to create undesirable tension paths. Cubic curve segments were better suited for the
task, where the tension curve would be continuous and smooth throughout the beam length.
Based on these types of curve segments, the program has been made to auto-generate the beam
geometry. This is based exclusively on the beam length, width, height, and supports as well
as a desired number of elements to represent each curve segment. This has been found to be
a great way to create the geometry, instead of the user having to manually create the beam
geometry - a tedious process for beams of many elements and curve segments. It does however
have a few drawbacks. The user is unable to add element nodes manually if they need a point
load at a certain value of x.
Friction loss and anchorage loss is added to the program, see section 4.3 which has been evaluated
in section 5.2 and 4.3.2 and discussed in section 7.4. The friction loss was accurate in each
element node, but could result in a very slight discrepancy in the sectional forces with curve
segments with inﬂection points in between element nodes. There is also a slight inaccuracy in
97
==PAGE:110==
the assumption that the curve length is the same as the length of the beam to distance x, but
nothing which would impact the sectional force calculations for shallow curves. The anchorage
loss calculated in the program is a good approximation for the analytically calculated anchorage
loss, but with few elements in the curve segments with anchorage loss, improvements could be
made, see section 7.4.
It was a challenge to implement the optimizers in the program. To get the optimizer to keep the
tension curve paths within the beam dimensions, or other boundaries needed, a new minimizer
which runs like fminsearch was found on the MathWorks File Exchange [10], fminsearchbnd.
This could create upper and lower boundaries for the input variable of fminsearch. This could
only keep the curve nodes within the beam boundaries. To improve on this, additional curve
nodes could be made in the beam geometry. This would increase the run time of the optimizers.
A possible improvement on the boundary problem is to create a completely new curve generator,
see section 7.6.
98
==PAGE:111==

[a b c d] =getCurve(kp)

function PXanchor=getAnchor(X,T,nel,Angl,ABCD,PX,PXst,PXsl,P0start)

%respectively.

% [Pa]
Steel strength

% [m2]

% [−]

notEqual=0;

notEqual=1;

end
